B007ELN8Y8|4.0|There's always a risk with a movie like \\"The Artist\\" that it will become all about the gimmick and nothing more.  But Michel Hazanavicius's modern-day re-creation of a black-and-white silent film is so adroitly executed and artistically wrought that it transcends the gimmick and becomes an impressive work in its own right.<br /><br />Set in the late `20s and early `30s, the period when moviemaking was transitioning from silents to talkies, the movie focuses on George Valentin (Jean Dujardin), a dapper matinee-idol who `s more devoted to his dog - the scene-stealing Uggie - than to his acting partner/wife (Penelope Ann Miller).  The problem is that Valentin seems more suited to the silent screen than the sound one - we learn why at the end - so we watch as his career and his personal life take a turn for the worse throughout the course of the story.  If that sounds a bit like the premise of \\"Singin' in the Rain,\\" you're right, but the movie also has a touch of \\"A Star is Born\\" about it, for as Valentin's career goes into an ever-increasing death-spiral, the beautiful young ingenue (Berenice Bejo) he helped to discover has taken to the talkies like gang-busters and is becoming a major star of the silver screen.  (One can also find a couple of homages to \\"Citizen Kane,\\" but they're a little less obvious).<br /><br />\\"The Artist\\" is obviously of most interest to those with a love of cinema and movie history, as it re-creates in breathtakingly beautiful terms the look and sound of silent movies (the tinkling-piano score is by Ludovic Bource).  The movie is pure joy to look at, thanks to the eye-popping cinematography of Guillaume Schiffman, which captures the otherworldly elegance of a world framed in black-and-white images. The story may strike some as facile and simplistic to say the least, but that's clearly in keeping with the temper of the times and the era of motion picture history in which it is set.  I could have done with a little more of the behind-the-camera glimpses into old-time moviemaking and a little less of the foreground romance, but that is a minor quibble in a movie that works on so many other levels.  One of those is the acting, with Dujardin and Bejo managing to create effective characters with only a minimum of mugging, and John Goodman and James Cromwell doing the same in supporting roles.  And little Uggie, who also appeared in \\"Mr. Fix-it\\" and \\"Water for Elephants,\\" puts Toto and Rin Tin Tin to shame in the canine-acting division.<br /><br />With movies like \\"Moneyball,\\" \\"The Descendents\\" and \\"The Tree of Life\\" hanging around, I wouldn't have voted for \\"The Artist\\" as the Best Picture of 2011, but I wouldn't have missed the one-of-a-kind visual experience it affords for anything in the world.limpses into old-time moviemaking and a little less of the foreground romance, but that is a minor quibble in a movie that works on so many other levels. This makes them more both more realistic and almost majestic.  It really gives the whole world a living/breathing quality and really sucks you in.<br /><br /> c. Size of the Colossus. If only the above issues could've been fixed, we'd be looking at a 9-9.5 game.  But, its really more of an 8-8.5.  Still a rare game thats greater than the sum of its parts.

The action is really cool and (sort of spoiler) kind of reminds me of Akira at some points.<br /><br />Overall it's a terrific little gem and I think they could certainly expand the universe with a sequel. B0021L8UQ2|5.0|I was honestly floored when I saw critics call this film \\"unrealistic\\" due to the fact that everyone seems to trust Esther (the psychopath homicidal girl) over her truth-telling but seemingly paranoid mother and a typical \\"It's always the parents' fault, not the child's\\" incompetent but far-from-uncommon therapist. Most horror films suffer from intense unbelievability, but this is definitely not one of them. It's only because *we* see the truth do we wonder how everyone can believe Esther over her mother. What no one seems to grasp is that they *WANT* to believe what they say, and the motivations every character has are definitely not unreasonable--if they took the mother's word, they'd be jumping into blaming the child for such horrific things, and it would almost invariably end in a downward spiral that would tear an already-salvaged family apart.<br /><br />I was impressed with the realism of every character. They even use the pathology \\"antisocial personality disorder\\" properly, and a totally believable family therapist who, like many other incompetent therapists, immediately blames the parents, not the children, for their problems. A therapist blaming the mother for her child's behavior is par for the course for many of these therapists, despite any and all evidence that the child is simply unruly, even with events that happen because of Esther.<br /><br />The two biological family members, Max and Daniel, have been criticized for being unrealistic, for not talking after seeing Esther do such horrific things. This is a mistake by critics. These are young children we're talking about who are, naturally, terrified of Esther, who threatens them with such intimidation that they don't care about anything except keeping her away from them, which means keeping quiet under any and all circumstances. Such young children can't understand that type of situation and the seriousness of it, and I really don't think it's such a stretch to say they would do the simplest thingand avoid Esther entirely so they don't get hurt, rather than tell someone and risk Esther doing the terrifying things she says she'll do if they talk. If she said the same things to adults, their thinking would be more adult-like, but they're very small children not even in puberty.<br /><br />Esther is also not an unbelievable character, and everyone's unwillingness to blame her despite that she *probably* had something to do with all of the happenings around her is perfectly natural. And the reason for that is because there's really no *actual* evidence that she was involved, and it would be unfair to blame an otherwise well-behaved child, and an orphan with a troubled past, at that. No one would be comfortable going against the quick consensus that the mother is just transferring her own guilt onto Esther. Not even her husband would be able to get past that in the real world. In terms of credibility, Esther simply wins by a landside. The mother is a recovering alcoholic and has periods of mental instability. Esther is a well-behaved, intelligent, and creative orphan who simply knows how to manipulate others behind their backs. She knows that they'll blame the mother the more she complains about Esther for things she can't find real evidence for. Esther is smart. The mother has no chance of winning the credibility war with no real evidence against Esther.<br /><br />Anyway, people who are complaining about how there's no way Esther could do the things she does and also that other kids would cover for her out of fear are mistaken. None of that is unrealistic given the context. The mother already starts off as someone to be watched for instability, and already lacks credibility before they even take Esther in. When her mother insists Esther is responsible for the bizarre happenings going on, we're talking about a not-so-perfect unstable mother against a well-behaved, creative child who knows exactly how to cover her own tracks, and in fact likes that her mother isso enraged about it, which only helps her own stability come off as credible.<br /><br />This is really an excellent thriller, one of my favorites. Esther is a true psychopath to the letter, and the way the film treats the nature of psychopathy is exceptional. It's a believable story, and the two hours rocket by while we watch Esther's psychopathy manifest more and more, and the suspense never lets up. Extremely highly recommended for horror and thriller fans.
